it's virtually impossible to classify the thoroughly unique entity that is The Roosevelts, this statement by guitarist Jason Kloess certainly tells part of the story. As one half of the electric duo along with singer James Mason, the two brothers in song - not blood, though maybe beards - have been playing together for years and cultivating a sound that's a little bluesy, a little bit country, a little bit rock &lsquo;n' roll. Sure, there's some mandolin in there. But most importantly, it's just heartfelt, genuine music that belongs to them alone, not any genre.\n\n \n\nKloess, who had begun his musical journey on piano, and Mason, who \"caught the bug\" in high school, bonded in Austin over a mutual love of the same influences: the song writing of James Taylor, the keen lyricism of Ryan Adams, the heartfelt soul of Joe Cocker. Their collection of songs showcases their strengths as a unit: smart, soulful grooves with crisp harmonies and sweetly infectious refrains that could fill living rooms just as easily as stadiums.\n\n \n\nBut The Roosevelts actually almost never came to be. Mason was ready to purse a career in medicine, pondering graduate school after working as a medic on ambulances, and Jason was developing a career in Austin's tech start up community.\n\n \n\n\"The hardest part of letting go of medicine for music, was the loss of the sensation that I was actually helping people,\" Mason says. \"Although I'm not saving lives with music, I have found that this platform actually allows me to connect with people on a deeper level. Relationships are the inspiration behind most of our songs, and of course, some of the easiest relationships to write about are romantic ones. But the inspiration is not always romantic. It's about connecting with people.\"\n\n \n\n\"We hope our music will break your heart and make you shout for joy, all at the same time,\" says Kloess. And it certainly will.\n\nThey've followed their natural inclination to write songs that tap into pure emotion shaped with jubilant musicianship and introspective lyrics. And they've built a devoted fan base along the way - a fan base that Mason and Kloess often make sure to chat with on a first-name basis out in the crowd as much as from the stage.\n\n \n\nFor their fans, the healing comes in the music - cathartic, joyful, full of life and light. There were two of them, and two of us. &#8211; I like to think they would have been fans of our music.&#8221; As one half of the electric duo along with lead guitarist Jason Kloess, the two brothers &#8211; in song, not blood (though maybe beards) &#8211; carved a distinctive niche on the renowned live music scene of Austin, Texas, before their recent move to the music industry&#8217;s &#8220;third coast&#8221; &#8211; Nashville, Tennessee. Together, the songsmiths cultivated a sound that&#39;s a little bluesy, a little bit folky, and a little bit rock &#39;n&#39; roll. Sure, there&#39;s some mandolin in there, but most importantly, it&#39;s just heartfelt, genuine music that belongs to them alone, not any genre. When forced to give it a name, the boys will tell you it&#8217;s &#8220;harmony-driven American rock with a 70s sensibility.&#8221;&nbsp;In the ongoing collapse of our collective attention span, the modern indie band at the 5th-album mark draws closer to endangered species status. Rarer still are bands who have managed to maintain the same integrity of creative purpose as&nbsp;Frontier Ruckus.Enter the Kingdom, their 5th and most lush record to date, serves as an almost desperate invitation into the band&#8217;s most recurrent setting: the suburban American household. It is immediately apparent, however, that the emphasis this time is not so much on idyllic nostalgia but the very real and present tense disintegration of a personal kingdom once thought permanent.We are thrust into stained living rooms where dads search for work on Craigslist, carports prowled by drunken ex-spouses returning with dubious motives, megachurch rec rooms marked by lust and disrepair.Songwriter&nbsp;Matthew Milia&nbsp;has explained the album as a rather literal depiction of his father losing his job and relying on disability checks to retain a tenuous grasp on his childhood home.
